	<div class="pure-g">
		<!-- ngIf: ::vm.details.impactStatement || vm.details.abstract || vm.details.scope || vm.details.purpose  || vm.details.previewImage --><div ng-if="::vm.details.impactStatement || vm.details.abstract || vm.details.scope || vm.details.purpose  || vm.details.previewImage" ng-class="vm.details.graphicalAbstract ? 'pure-u-14-24 u-pr-1' : 'pure-u-1-1'" class="ng-scope pure-u-1-1">
			<!-- ngIf: ::vm.details.impactStatement -->
		<!--/div-->
		<!--div ng-class="{'pure-u-14-24 u-pr-1':  vm.details.graphicalAbstract, 'pure-u-1-1': ! vm.details.graphicalAbstract}"-->
			<!-- ngIf: ::vm.details.abstract --><div ng-if="::vm.details.abstract" class="ng-scope">
				<strong>Abstract:</strong>
				<div class="abstract-text ng-binding" ng-bind-html="::vm.details.abstract">Increasing backscatter tag communication ranges is crucial for the development of low-power long-range wireless sensor networks. A major limitation for increasing the signal-to-noise ratio (SNR) for RF identification tags lies in the fact that tag antennas are terminated with passive loads for modulation, which yields reflection-coefficient values less than unity. Recent work in the field has exploited reflection amplifiers that achieve reflection-coefficient values larger than unity to increase the communication range. However, most of these systems rely on increasing the reflection coefficient at one modulation state only, which is suboptimal. In this paper, an analysis is given for the optimal way to utilize a reflection amplifier and how this compares to suboptimal practices. To demonstrate the concept, a tag is designed that achieves reflection-coefficient values higher than unity for both states in the 900-930-MHz band. The two values are antipodal, thus maximizing the tag SNR for a given amplifier. The system comprises of an ultra-low-power reflection amplifier with up to 10.2-dB gain and sub-milliwatt power consumption, and a phase-shift modulator that selectively alternates the phase of the backscatter signal between 0 <sup>°</sup> and 180 <sup>°</sup>. The reflection amplifier-phase modulator system is experimentally characterized in terms of gain, power consumption, and backscatter efficiency.</div>
			</div><!-- end ngIf: ::vm.details.abstract -->			
			<!-- ngIf: ::vm.details.scope -->

The free oscillations caused by any normal variations in tube ...&quot;,&quot;openUrlImgLoc&quot;:&quot;http://staticieeexplore.ieee.org/assets/img/btn.find-in-library.png&quot;,&quot;pdfSize&quot;:&quot;3372KB&quot;},&quot;refType&quot;:&quot;biblio&quot;,&quot;id&quot;:&quot;ref23&quot;},{&quot;order&quot;:&quot;24&quot;,&quot;text&quot;:&quot;S.-J. Workshop&lt;/em&gt;, pp. 153-161, 2013-Sep.&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;A remotely programmable modular\ntestbed for backscatter sensor network research&quot;,&quot;context&quot;:[{&quot;sec&quot;:&quot;sec5&quot;,&quot;text&quot;:&quot; Experiments in [31] have shown that the RF clutter might occupy a bandwidth of up to 100 kHz, and low-SNR tag signals in that frequency region may be completely “hidden” from the reader.&quot;,&quot;part&quot;:&quot;1&quot;}],&quot;links&quot;:{&quot;crossRefLink&quot;:&quot;https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-319-03071-5_17&quot;,&quot;openUrlImgLoc&quot;:&quot;http://staticieeexplore.ieee.org/assets/img/btn.find-in-library.png&quot;},&quot;refType&quot;:&quot;biblio&quot;,&quot;id&quot;:&quot;ref31&quot;},{&quot;order&quot;:&quot;32&quot;,&quot;text&quot;:&quot;W. two-ray path loss model that accounts for a line-of-sight path and a strong reflection from the ground with the formula [32] where \\$G_{T}, G_{R}\\$ are the tag antenna and reader antenna gain values, respectively, \\$h_{T}, h_{R}\\$ are the heights of the tag and reader antenna placement with respect to ground, and \\$d\\$ is the distance between the tag and the <div id="BodyWrapper" class="ArticlePage" xmlns:ieee="http://www.ieeexplore.ieee.org"><div id="article">
<div class="section" id="sec1"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION I.</div><h2>Introduction</h2></div><p>Although the first backscatter radio principles were introduced six decades ago <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref1" id="context_ref_1_1">[1]</a>, the architecture of most backscatter modulators for RF tags [e.g., RF identification (RFID)] has followed the same pattern; alternating the tag antenna termination between two passive load values to achieve modulation by modifying the reflection of radio waves. Active components (transistors, RF switches, diodes) are introduced to the tag RF front-ends, but are just utilized as mechanisms to switch the antenna load between two states for achieving backscatter modulation. The trend of switching between two load values only has been recently challenged in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref2" id="context_ref_2_1">[2]</a> and <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref3" id="context_ref_3_1">[3]</a>, but the loads used are still passive, limiting the RF tag scattering efficiency. Scattering efficiency refers to the amount of power a tag can reflect for a given induced power level, in order to perform tag-to-reader communication <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref4" id="context_ref_4_1">[4]</a>. Improving the performance of the backscatter link is crucial for achieving a long tag-to-reader communication range. The latter is required for the development of ultra-low-cost and ultra-low-power wireless sensor networks (WSNs) that may utilize backscatter radio instead of active power-hungry radios. Backscatter radio for WSNs has received much attention in the literature <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref5" id="context_ref_5_1">[5]</a>–<a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref6" id="context_ref_6_1">[6]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref7" id="context_ref_7_1">[7]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref8" id="context_ref_8_1">[8]</a>, and thus extended ranges constitute a very important design constraint, along with the utilization of energy harvesting, especially from ambient energy sources <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref9" id="context_ref_9_1">[9]</a>–<a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref10" id="context_ref_10_1">[10]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref11" id="context_ref_11_1">[11]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref12" id="context_ref_12_1">[12]</a>. Commercial RFID system architectures, based on all-passive tags and monostatic reader architectures, have been proven to achieve very limited ranges <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref13" id="context_ref_13_1">[13]</a>, and thus are inappropriate for long-range sensing.</p><p>When aiming for long communication ranges of several tens of meters, minimization of the tag bit error rate (BER) at the reader is required, which is associated with the tag-scattered signal's signal-to-noise ratio (SNR). In <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref14" id="context_ref_14_1">[14]</a>, where the complete backscatter radio signal model is derived by accounting for both communication theory and microwave theory, it can be seen that the tag signal SNR at the reader is <disp-formula id="deqn1" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{\rm SNR} \triangleq {{E_{b}}\over {N_{0}}}={{P_{\rm tag}~T_{b}}\over {N_{0}}}\propto\vert\Delta\Gamma\vert^{2}~T_{b}.\eqno{\hbox{(1)}}</span><script type="math/tex; mode=display">{\rm SNR} \triangleq {{E_{b}}\over {N_{0}}}={{P_{\rm tag}~T_{b}}\over {N_{0}}}\propto\vert\Delta\Gamma\vert^{2}~T_{b}.\eqno{\hbox{(1)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">{\rm SNR} \triangleq {{E_{b}}\over {N_{0}}}={{P_{\rm tag}~T_{b}}\over {N_{0}}}\propto\vert\Delta\Gamma\vert^{2}~T_{b}.\eqno{\hbox{(1)}}</span></span></disp-formula> <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">E_{b}</span><script type="math/tex">E_{b}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the bit energy, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">P_{\rm tag}</span><script type="math/tex">P_{\rm tag}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the average power of the received tag signal at the reader, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">T_{b}</span><script type="math/tex">T_{b}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the bit duration, and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">N_{0}</span><script type="math/tex">N_{0}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the noise power spectral density. The tag-signal's energy is proportional to <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\vert \Delta \Gamma \vert ^{2}\triangleq\vert\Gamma _{1}-\Gamma_{0}\vert^{2}</span><script type="math/tex">\vert \Delta \Gamma \vert ^{2}\triangleq\vert\Gamma _{1}-\Gamma_{0}\vert^{2}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, where <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Gamma_{0}, \Gamma_{1}</span><script type="math/tex">\Gamma_{0}, \Gamma_{1}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> are the complex reflection-coefficient values for the two load values a backscatter tag modulator is using. In <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref4" id="context_ref_4_1">[4]</a>, it has been stated that maximization of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\vert \Delta \Gamma \vert</span><script type="math/tex">\vert \Delta \Gamma \vert</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is required for improving the RF tag scattering efficiency and minimizing the BER at the reader, regardless of the binary modulation scheme utilized. All modulation schemes can benefit from that efficiency increase; amplitude-shift keying (ASK) waveforms will have higher amplitude values, phase-shift keying (PSK) waveforms will employ signal bases with the maximum possible phase difference, and frequency-shift keying (FSK) waveforms will feature an increased waveform amplitude, positively affecting signal demodulation at the reader. The efficient tag demodulation at the reader is translated to an uplink (tag-to-reader) communication range boost, which can be further enhanced by utilizing other-than-monostatic reader architectures <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref14" id="context_ref_14_1">[14]</a>. Since RF tags typically utilize passive components for their load modulators, they achieve reflection coefficients <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Gamma _{0}, \Gamma _{1}</span><script type="math/tex">\Gamma _{0}, \Gamma _{1}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> with magnitude values less or equal to unity. Thus, all typical load modulators' performance is upper bounded by <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\vert \Delta \Gamma \vert \leq 2</span><script type="math/tex">\vert \Delta \Gamma \vert \leq 2</script></tex-math></inline-formula> with the equality achieved for coefficients with maximum magnitude (unity) and phase difference <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\pi</span><script type="math/tex">\pi</script></tex-math></inline-formula> or 180° (antipodal points on the Smith chart). This upper bound is limiting backscatter signal SNR maximization and nonconventional tag designs have to be exploited to override this bound. Specifically, to achieve values of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\vert \Gamma \vert&gt;1</span><script type="math/tex">\vert \Gamma \vert&gt;1</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, active topologies are required that have a reflection gain rather than reflection loss.</p><p>A reflection amplifier has been previously proposed for 5.8-GHz RFID tags to amplify the backscattered signal towards the reader by achieving a reflection coefficient higher than unity at one modulation state <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">(\vert \Gamma \vert &gt; 1)</span><script type="math/tex">(\vert \Gamma \vert &gt; 1)</script></tex-math></inline-formula> <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref15" id="context_ref_15_1">[15]</a>. In that work, the modulation at the amplifier output is achieved by controlling (switching on and off) the amplifier bias; as it will be shown later in this paper, this operation is suboptimal. Moreover that amplifier has been designed without setting strong constraints for the dissipation and requires a few milliamperes for operation. In <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref16" id="context_ref_16_1">[16]</a>, an active 24-GHz tag was proposed, that achieved up to 15 m by transmitting pseudorandom sequences generated by active antennas with integrated oscillators. However, that system was radiating signals towards a reader rather than reflecting reader-emitted signals and it required 2.5 V and 26 mA for operation. In <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref17" id="context_ref_17_1">[17]</a>, a 34.45-GHz tag based on a switched injection-locked oscillator was designed that achieved up to 11.5-m range. That work is more similar to the concept of a system that receives a reader signal, amplifies it, and reflects it, such as in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref15" id="context_ref_15_1">[15]</a> and this paper. It is noted, though, that the front-end incorporates a low-noise amplifier (LNA), power amplifiers (PAs), and complex circuitry for phase locking. The system requires 3 V for operation and its current consumption goes up to <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{\hbox{40}}\sim {\hbox {50}}~{\hbox {mA}}</span><script type="math/tex">{\hbox{40}}\sim {\hbox {50}}~{\hbox {mA}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, which is much higher than the requirement of this work's amplifier.</p><p>In this paper, a system architecture is presented that utilizes a reflection amplifier and a phase shifting modulator (<a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig1" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 1</a>, right) to achieve reflection-coefficient amplitudes greater than unity for both tag states. The dissipated power of the amplifier is minimized in order to be effectively powered by low-voltage sources such as small batteries, solar cells, or charge tanks. The modulator is designed to perform a 0° or 180° phase shifting on the backscattered signal, thus achieving binary phase shift keying (BPSK) modulation, with antipodal signal bases of the same amplitude, optimizing (maximizing) the <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\vert \Delta \Gamma \vert &gt; 2</span><script type="math/tex">\vert \Delta \Gamma \vert &gt; 2</script></tex-math></inline-formula> factor for a given reflection amplifier gain. This maximizes the SNR of the backscattered signal at the reader, significantly enhancing the communication range of an RF tag.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig1"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/6971048/6939737/6939737-fig-1-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/6971048/6939737/6939737-fig-1-small.gif" alt="Figure 1" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 1. </b><fig><p>(Left) conventional backscatter RF tag modulator. (Right) proposed tag with reflection amplifier and binary phase-shift modulator.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/6939737/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<div class="figure figure-full" id="fig2"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/6971048/6939737/6939737-fig-2-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/6971048/6939737/6939737-fig-2-small.gif" alt="Figure 2" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 2. </b><fig><p>For a tag with a conventional load modulator, both reflection-coefficient points lie in the smith chart. In the ideal case, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Gamma _{0}</span><script type="math/tex">\Gamma _{0}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Gamma _{1}</span><script type="math/tex">\Gamma _{1}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> are antipodal.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/6939737/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<p></p><p>This work is organized as follows. <a ref-type="sec" anchor="sec2" class="fulltext-link">Section II</a> theoretically analyzes the constraints for increased scattering efficiency. <a ref-type="sec" anchor="sec3" class="fulltext-link">Section III</a> describes the design, implementation, and characterization of the reflection amplifier. <a ref-type="sec" anchor="sec4" class="fulltext-link">Section IV</a> describes the design, implementation, and performance of the phase-shifting modulator, and <a ref-type="sec" anchor="sec5" class="fulltext-link">Section V</a> discusses the evaluation of the whole system. Finally, <a ref-type="sec" anchor="sec6" class="fulltext-link">Section VI</a> offers conclusion of this work.</p></div>
<div class="section" id="sec2"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION II.</div><h2>Increasing Scattering Efficiency</h2></div><p>A typical backscatter radio modulator connected to an antenna with input impedance <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">Z_{a}</span><script type="math/tex">Z_{a}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is switching the antenna termination between two load values <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">Z_{0}, Z_{1}</span><script type="math/tex">Z_{0}, Z_{1}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> (<a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig1" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 1</a>, left) for bits “0” and “1,” respectively <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref13" id="context_ref_13_2">[13]</a>. In that way, two different reflection-coefficient values <disp-formula id="deqn2" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Gamma_{0}={{Z_{0}-Z_{a}^{\ast}}\over{Z_{0} +Z_{a}}},\quad\Gamma_{1}={{Z_{1}-Z_{a}^{\ast}}\over {Z_{1} + Z_{a}}} \eqno{\hbox{(2)}}</span><script type="math/tex; mode=display">\Gamma_{0}={{Z_{0}-Z_{a}^{\ast}}\over{Z_{0} +Z_{a}}},\quad\Gamma_{1}={{Z_{1}-Z_{a}^{\ast}}\over {Z_{1} + Z_{a}}} \eqno{\hbox{(2)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">\Gamma_{0}={{Z_{0}-Z_{a}^{\ast}}\over{Z_{0} +Z_{a}}},\quad\Gamma_{1}={{Z_{1}-Z_{a}^{\ast}}\over {Z_{1} + Z_{a}}} \eqno{\hbox{(2)}}</span></span></disp-formula>are achieved and the amplitude and/or phase of the backscattered signal are altered <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref4" id="context_ref_4_2">[4]</a>, <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref18" id="context_ref_18_2">[18]</a>. The load value selection will affect the reflection-coefficient values and different modulation schemes can be achieved: ASK, PSK, or hybrids. Switching between the two loads multiple times per bit period can yield an FSK signal <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref14" id="context_ref_14_2">[14]</a>, while switching between <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">M</span><script type="math/tex">M</script></tex-math></inline-formula> load values can yield high-order modulation schemes, such as <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">M</span><script type="math/tex">M</script></tex-math></inline-formula>-QAM <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref2" id="context_ref_2_2">[2]</a>. The backscattered signal constellation is directly related to the points of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Gamma _{0}, \Gamma _{1}</span><script type="math/tex">\Gamma _{0}, \Gamma _{1}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> on the Smith chart. For example, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Gamma_{0}=-1</span><script type="math/tex">\Gamma_{0}=-1</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Gamma_{1} = 1</span><script type="math/tex">\Gamma_{1} = 1</script></tex-math></inline-formula> would correspond to BPSK modulation since only the phase of the backscatter signal changes for the two states, while the amplitude remains the same. The same holds for any two antipodal points on the unitary circle (<a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig2" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 2</a>). Notice that for any passive load <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">Z_{x}</span><script type="math/tex">Z_{x}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, the corresponding system reflection coefficient will always have magnitude <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\vert \Gamma _{x}\vert \leq 1</span><script type="math/tex">\vert \Gamma _{x}\vert \leq 1</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. The limitation of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\vert \Gamma _{x}\vert \leq 1</span><script type="math/tex">\vert \Gamma _{x}\vert \leq 1</script></tex-math></inline-formula> can be overridden by utilizing a reflection amplifier, which is a negative resistance oscillator <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref19" id="context_ref_19_2">[19]</a>, sub-biased in a way that no oscillations occur.</p><p>The antipodal load modulator in a classic backscatter radio tag (<a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig1" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 1</a>, left) that maximizes <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\vert \Delta \Gamma \vert</span><script type="math/tex">\vert \Delta \Gamma \vert</script></tex-math></inline-formula> switches between two reflection coefficients <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Gamma_{0},\Gamma_{1}</span><script type="math/tex">\Gamma_{0},\Gamma_{1}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. These reflection coefficients and their difference magnitude are given by <disp-formula id="deqn3-5" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\eqalignno{\Gamma _{0}=&amp;\,1~e^{j\theta} &amp;{\hbox{(3)}}\cr\Gamma_{1}=&amp;\,1~e^{j\theta+ j\pi}=-1~e^{j\theta}=-\Gamma_{0}&amp;{\hbox{(4)}}\cr\vert\Delta\Gamma \vert=&amp;\, \vert e^{j\theta}+e^{j\theta}\vert=2 &amp;{\hbox{(5)}}}</span><script type="math/tex; mode=display">\eqalignno{\Gamma _{0}=&amp;\,1~e^{j\theta} &amp;{\hbox{(3)}}\cr\Gamma_{1}=&amp;\,1~e^{j\theta+ j\pi}=-1~e^{j\theta}=-\Gamma_{0}&amp;{\hbox{(4)}}\cr\vert\Delta\Gamma \vert=&amp;\, \vert e^{j\theta}+e^{j\theta}\vert=2 &amp;{\hbox{(5)}}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">\eqalignno{\Gamma _{0}=&amp;\,1~e^{j\theta} &amp;{\hbox{(3)}}\cr\Gamma_{1}=&amp;\,1~e^{j\theta+ j\pi}=-1~e^{j\theta}=-\Gamma_{0}&amp;{\hbox{(4)}}\cr\vert\Delta\Gamma \vert=&amp;\, \vert e^{j\theta}+e^{j\theta}\vert=2 &amp;{\hbox{(5)}}}</span></span></disp-formula>with <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\theta \in [0, 2\pi)</span><script type="math/tex">\theta \in [0, 2\pi)</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. This well-known case is depicted on a Smith chart in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig2" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 2</a>, where both coefficients lie on the unitary circle. Notice that passive tags (e.g., RFIDs) that employ antenna-matched harvesters (i.e., the harvester input impedance is <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">Z_{\rm harv}= Z_{a}^{\ast}</span><script type="math/tex">Z_{\rm harv}= Z_{a}^{\ast}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> to maximize power transfer from the antenna to the harvester module) will switch between a matched and an unmatched state, i.e., <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Gamma_{0}=0</span><script type="math/tex">\Gamma_{0}=0</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Gamma_{1}=1 e^{j\theta}</span><script type="math/tex">\Gamma_{1}=1 e^{j\theta}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, achieving a distance <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\vert \Delta \Gamma\vert = 1</span><script type="math/tex">\vert \Delta \Gamma\vert = 1</script></tex-math></inline-formula>.</p><p>
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig3"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/6971048/6939737/6939737-fig-3-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/6971048/6939737/6939737-fig-3-small.gif" alt="Figure 3" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 3. </b><fig><p>For a reflection amplifier tag that switches the amplifier on and off, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Gamma _{0}</span><script type="math/tex">\Gamma _{0}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> (“off”-state) lies inside the Smith chart, while <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Gamma _{1}</span><script type="math/tex">\Gamma _{1}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> (“on”-state) lies away from the unitary circle (reflection gain).</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/6939737/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
Next, consider a system that consists of a reflection amplifier with power gain <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">G</span><script type="math/tex">G</script></tex-math></inline-formula> directly interfaced to the antenna (as in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref15" id="context_ref_15_2">[15]</a>). The amplifier is switched on and off to achieve ASK modulation. When the amplifier is off, the equivalent circuit seen by the antenna port is a passive load. The system reflection coefficient will have amplitude <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">B \leq 1</span><script type="math/tex">B \leq 1</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and arbitrary phase <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\phi _{0}</span><script type="math/tex">\phi _{0}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. When on, the amplifier shows a reflection voltage gain <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\sqrt {G}</span><script type="math/tex">\sqrt {G}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and applies a phase <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\phi _{1}</span><script type="math/tex">\phi _{1}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> to the incoming signal. Thus, <disp-formula id="deqn6-7" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\eqalignno{\Gamma_{0}=&amp;\,B~e^{j\phi _{0}}&amp;{\hbox{(6)}}\cr\Gamma_{1}=&amp;\,\sqrt{G}~e^{j\phi_{1}}.&amp;{\hbox{(7)}}}</span><script type="math/tex; mode=display">\eqalignno{\Gamma_{0}=&amp;\,B~e^{j\phi _{0}}&amp;{\hbox{(6)}}\cr\Gamma_{1}=&amp;\,\sqrt{G}~e^{j\phi_{1}}.&amp;{\hbox{(7)}}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">\eqalignno{\Gamma_{0}=&amp;\,B~e^{j\phi _{0}}&amp;{\hbox{(6)}}\cr\Gamma_{1}=&amp;\,\sqrt{G}~e^{j\phi_{1}}.&amp;{\hbox{(7)}}}</span></span></disp-formula> Two such reflection-coefficient points are shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig3" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 3</a>. The reflection-coefficient difference amplitude is <disp-formula id="deqn8" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\vert\Delta\Gamma\vert=\vert\sqrt{G}~e^{j\phi _{1}}-B~e^{j\phi_{0}}\vert.\eqno{\hbox{(8)}}</span><script type="math/tex; mode=display">\vert\Delta\Gamma\vert=\vert\sqrt{G}~e^{j\phi _{1}}-B~e^{j\phi_{0}}\vert.\eqno{\hbox{(8)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">\vert\Delta\Gamma\vert=\vert\sqrt{G}~e^{j\phi _{1}}-B~e^{j\phi_{0}}\vert.\eqno{\hbox{(8)}}</span></span></disp-formula>The distance <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\vert \Delta \Gamma \vert</span><script type="math/tex">\vert \Delta \Gamma \vert</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is maximized (best case) when <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">B=1</span><script type="math/tex">B=1</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and the line that connects the two points crosses the axis origin <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">(0,0)</span><script type="math/tex">(0,0)</script></tex-math></inline-formula>.<p></p><p>This is achieved when <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\phi_{1} = \phi_{0}+\pi</span><script type="math/tex">\phi_{1} = \phi_{0}+\pi</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. Then <disp-formula id="deqn9" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\vert\Delta\Gamma\vert=\vert\sqrt{G}~e^{j\phi_{0} + j\pi}-e^{j\phi_{0}}\vert=\sqrt{G}+ 1.\eqno{\hbox{(9)}}</span><script type="math/tex; mode=display">\vert\Delta\Gamma\vert=\vert\sqrt{G}~e^{j\phi_{0} + j\pi}-e^{j\phi_{0}}\vert=\sqrt{G}+ 1.\eqno{\hbox{(9)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">\vert\Delta\Gamma\vert=\vert\sqrt{G}~e^{j\phi_{0} + j\pi}-e^{j\phi_{0}}\vert=\sqrt{G}+ 1.\eqno{\hbox{(9)}}</span></span></disp-formula> Similarly, the distance is minimized (worst case) when <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">B=1</span><script type="math/tex">B=1</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\phi _{1} = \phi _{0}</span><script type="math/tex">\phi _{1} = \phi _{0}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. Then <disp-formula id="deqn10" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\vert\Delta\Gamma\vert=\vert\sqrt{G}~e^{j\phi_{0}}-e^{j\phi_{0}}\vert=\sqrt{G}-1.\eqno{\hbox{(10)}}</span><script type="math/tex; mode=display">\vert\Delta\Gamma\vert=\vert\sqrt{G}~e^{j\phi_{0}}-e^{j\phi_{0}}\vert=\sqrt{G}-1.\eqno{\hbox{(10)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">\vert\Delta\Gamma\vert=\vert\sqrt{G}~e^{j\phi_{0}}-e^{j\phi_{0}}\vert=\sqrt{G}-1.\eqno{\hbox{(10)}}</span></span></disp-formula></p><p>For the proposed system of <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig1" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 1</a> (right), consider a reflection amplifier with a gain <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">G</span><script type="math/tex">G</script></tex-math></inline-formula> that applies an arbitrary phase <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\phi_{1}</span><script type="math/tex">\phi_{1}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> to the incoming signal.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig4"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/6971048/6939737/6939737-fig-4-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/6971048/6939737/6939737-fig-4-small.gif" alt="Figure 4" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 4. </b><fig><p>For a reflection amplifier tag that utilizes a phase-shift modulator, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Gamma _{0}</span><script type="math/tex">\Gamma _{0}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Gamma _{1}</span><script type="math/tex">\Gamma _{1}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> can both lie far from the unitary circle. maximum distance between <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Gamma _{0}</span><script type="math/tex">\Gamma _{0}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Gamma _{1}</span><script type="math/tex">\Gamma _{1}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> can be achieved when these are diametrically opposed.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/6939737/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
If the amplifier is constantly on, then its complex <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">S_{11}</span><script type="math/tex">S_{11}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>-parameter is <disp-formula id="deqn11" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">S_{11}^{\rm amp}=\sqrt{G}~e^{j\phi_{1}}.\eqno{\hbox{(11)}}</span><script type="math/tex; mode=display">S_{11}^{\rm amp}=\sqrt{G}~e^{j\phi_{1}}.\eqno{\hbox{(11)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">S_{11}^{\rm amp}=\sqrt{G}~e^{j\phi_{1}}.\eqno{\hbox{(11)}}</span></span></disp-formula> Also, consider a two-port phase shifter modulator that can selectively shift the phase of the passing RF signal by 0 or <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\pi /2</span><script type="math/tex">\pi /2</script></tex-math></inline-formula> by directing it through a delay line, i.e., <disp-formula id="deqn12" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">S_{21}^{\rm mod}=\cases{1~e^{-j\pi/2},\qquad {\hbox{State 0}}\cr 1, \qquad {\hbox{State 1}}.}\eqno{\hbox{(12)}}</span><script type="math/tex; mode=display">S_{21}^{\rm mod}=\cases{1~e^{-j\pi/2},\qquad {\hbox{State 0}}\cr 1, \qquad {\hbox{State 1}}.}\eqno{\hbox{(12)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">S_{21}^{\rm mod}=\cases{1~e^{-j\pi/2},\qquad {\hbox{State 0}}\cr 1, \qquad {\hbox{State 1}}.}\eqno{\hbox{(12)}}</span></span></disp-formula> The total system input reflection coefficient will then be <disp-formula id="deqn13" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">S_{11}^{\rm system}= S_{21}^{\rm mod}~S_{11}^{\rm amp}~S_{21}^{\rm mod}=\cases{-\sqrt {G}~e^{j\phi _{1}}, \qquad {\hbox{State 0}}\cr\sqrt{G}~e^{j\phi_{1}},\qquad {\hbox{State 1}}.}\eqno{\hbox{(13)}}</span><script type="math/tex; mode=display">S_{11}^{\rm system}= S_{21}^{\rm mod}~S_{11}^{\rm amp}~S_{21}^{\rm mod}=\cases{-\sqrt {G}~e^{j\phi _{1}}, \qquad {\hbox{State 0}}\cr\sqrt{G}~e^{j\phi_{1}},\qquad {\hbox{State 1}}.}\eqno{\hbox{(13)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">S_{11}^{\rm system}= S_{21}^{\rm mod}~S_{11}^{\rm amp}~S_{21}^{\rm mod}=\cases{-\sqrt {G}~e^{j\phi _{1}}, \qquad {\hbox{State 0}}\cr\sqrt{G}~e^{j\phi_{1}},\qquad {\hbox{State 1}}.}\eqno{\hbox{(13)}}</span></span></disp-formula> Thus, the two reflection coefficients are <disp-formula id="deqn14-15" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\eqalignno{\Gamma _{0}=&amp;\,-\sqrt{G}~e^{j\phi _{1}}&amp;{\hbox{(14)}}\cr\Gamma _{1}=&amp;\,\sqrt{G}~e^{j\phi _{1}} = -\Gamma _{0}&amp;{\hbox{(15)}}}</span><script type="math/tex; mode=display">\eqalignno{\Gamma _{0}=&amp;\,-\sqrt{G}~e^{j\phi _{1}}&amp;{\hbox{(14)}}\cr\Gamma _{1}=&amp;\,\sqrt{G}~e^{j\phi _{1}} = -\Gamma _{0}&amp;{\hbox{(15)}}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">\eqalignno{\Gamma _{0}=&amp;\,-\sqrt{G}~e^{j\phi _{1}}&amp;{\hbox{(14)}}\cr\Gamma _{1}=&amp;\,\sqrt{G}~e^{j\phi _{1}} = -\Gamma _{0}&amp;{\hbox{(15)}}}</span></span></disp-formula>which both have an (equal) amplitude greater than unity, and thus, both lie away from the unitary smith chart circle (<a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig4" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 4</a>). Also, they are diametrically opposed, and the maximum possible reflection-coefficient distance for a given amplifier gain is achieved <disp-formula id="deqn16" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\vert\Delta\Gamma\vert=\vert\sqrt{G}~e^{j\phi_{1}}+\sqrt{G}~e^{j\phi_{1}}\vert= 2\sqrt {G}.\eqno{\hbox{(16)}}</span><script type="math/tex; mode=display">\vert\Delta\Gamma\vert=\vert\sqrt{G}~e^{j\phi_{1}}+\sqrt{G}~e^{j\phi_{1}}\vert= 2\sqrt {G}.\eqno{\hbox{(16)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">\vert\Delta\Gamma\vert=\vert\sqrt{G}~e^{j\phi_{1}}+\sqrt{G}~e^{j\phi_{1}}\vert= 2\sqrt {G}.\eqno{\hbox{(16)}}</span></span></disp-formula><p></p><p>In practice, the phase-shifting modulator will have a power transmission coefficient <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">0 &lt; T &lt; 1</span><script type="math/tex">0 &lt; T &lt; 1</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, and the achievable distance will be <disp-formula id="deqn17" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\vert\Delta\Gamma\vert^{\rm lossy}=\sqrt{T}~\vert\Delta\Gamma\vert~\sqrt{T}=2~T\sqrt{G}.\eqno{\hbox{(17)}}</span><script type="math/tex; mode=display">\vert\Delta\Gamma\vert^{\rm lossy}=\sqrt{T}~\vert\Delta\Gamma\vert~\sqrt{T}=2~T\sqrt{G}.\eqno{\hbox{(17)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">\vert\Delta\Gamma\vert^{\rm lossy}=\sqrt{T}~\vert\Delta\Gamma\vert~\sqrt{T}=2~T\sqrt{G}.\eqno{\hbox{(17)}}</span></span></disp-formula> When the factor <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">T</span><script type="math/tex">T</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is kept high (low insertion loss), the use of an amplifier-phase-shift modulator system will yield a higher <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\vert \Delta \Gamma \vert</span><script type="math/tex">\vert \Delta \Gamma \vert</script></tex-math></inline-formula> than an on–off amplifier system. When compared with the best-case on–off amplifier, a system of the proposed architecture is preferred when <disp-formula id="deqn18" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">2T\sqrt{G}&gt;\sqrt{G}+1\Leftrightarrow T &gt; {{1}\over {2}} + {{1}\over {2\sqrt {G}}}\eqno{\hbox{(18)}}</span><script type="math/tex; mode=display">2T\sqrt{G}&gt;\sqrt{G}+1\Leftrightarrow T &gt; {{1}\over {2}} + {{1}\over {2\sqrt {G}}}\eqno{\hbox{(18)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">2T\sqrt{G}&gt;\sqrt{G}+1\Leftrightarrow T &gt; {{1}\over {2}} + {{1}\over {2\sqrt {G}}}\eqno{\hbox{(18)}}</span></span></disp-formula> i.e., the insertion loss <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">10\log _{10}T</span><script type="math/tex">10\log _{10}T</script></tex-math></inline-formula> of the phase shifter has to be low. In <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig5" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 5</a>, the maximum acceptable insertion loss of the phase shifter is plotted versus the amplifier reflection gain. Intuitively, as the amplifier gain increases, the loss level that can be tolerated becomes higher. As an example, for an amplifier reflection gain of 10 dB, the maximum acceptable insertion loss is <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">-{\hbox {1.82 dB}}</span><script type="math/tex">-{\hbox {1.82 dB}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig5"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/6971048/6939737/6939737-fig-5-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/6971048/6939737/6939737-fig-5-small.gif" alt="Figure 5" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 5. </b><fig><p>Maximum acceptable phase-shifter loss for given reflection amplifier gain, to achieve tag SNR gain over conventional tags with load modulators.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/6939737/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<p></p></div>
<div class="section" id="sec3"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION III.</div><h2>Reflection Amplifier Design</h2></div><p>A reflection amplifier is a negative resistance amplifier, which uses a single port as both its input and output terminals. This property leads to compact low-profile circuits, and consequently has been proposed in active antenna implementations for RFID applications, such as in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref19" id="context_ref_19_3">[19]</a> and <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref20" id="context_ref_20_3">[20]</a> where 5.25- and 21-GHz tags utilizing reflection amplifiers are proposed. Furthermore, reflection amplifiers have been considered in active reflector circuits used as amplify and forward active antennas in wireless communication scenarios <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref21" id="context_ref_21_3">[21]</a>. The negative resistance amplifier concept is attributed to Armstrong <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref22" id="context_ref_22_3">[22]</a>, <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref23" id="context_ref_23_3">[23]</a>.</p><p>In the case of RFID architectures, a reflection amplifier can be used as a bidirectional amplifier in order to amplify both an incoming signal from the tag antenna towards the tag chip, as well as a reflected signal from the tag chip towards the tag antenna. A bidirectional reflection and transmission amplifier has been proposed in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref19" id="context_ref_19_3">[19]</a>, whereas in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref24" id="context_ref_24_3">[24]</a>, the combination of two reflection amplifiers and a directional hybrid was used to create a bidirectional amplifier topology used in a 6-GHz Van Atta retro-directive array. In <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref25" id="context_ref_25_3">[25]</a>, an ultra-high-frequency (UHF) RFID system was proposed that incorporates a coupler and a reflection amplifier circuit. It was shown that provided the amplifier has a minimum gain, the combination of the coupler and amplifier can behave as a bidirectional amplifier, thus leading to improved range of the RFID tag, while minimizing the required number of components and power consumption compared to <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref15" id="context_ref_15_3">[15]</a> and <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref24" id="context_ref_24_3">[24]</a>.</p><p>
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig6"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/6971048/6939737/6939737-fig-6-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/6971048/6939737/6939737-fig-6-small.gif" alt="Figure 6" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 6. </b><fig><p>Reflection amplifier schematic: negative resistance amplifier topology with a common input/output terminal (RF port). The amplifier is biased with a low DC voltage to prevent oscillations. an RF blocking filter is utilized at the bias point to prevent RF leakage to the DC supply.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/6939737/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
The design of reflection amplifier circuits is similar to oscillator design because the desired amplifier gain is obtained by generating a negative resistance without, however, fulfilling oscillating conditions. Consequently, a challenge in such circuits consists in controlling the stability of the circuit <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref15" id="context_ref_15_3">[15]</a>, <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref19" id="context_ref_19_3">[19]</a>, <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref21" id="context_ref_21_3">[21]</a>, as well as its bandwidth <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref26" id="context_ref_26_3">[26]</a>.<p></p><p>In this work, emphasis is placed on minimizing the dissipated power and maximizing the efficiency of the reflection amplifier, and for this reason its design was based on a Class-E oscillator topology. A Class-E oscillator is created by introducing appropriate feedback in a Class-E amplifier circuit, which leads to a high dc–RF efficiency. A Class-E power oscillator with 95% efficiency at 2 MHz and with 3-W output power was presented in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref27" id="context_ref_27_3">[27]</a>, while a microwave Class-E oscillator with 59% efficiency and 300-mW output power at 5 GHz was demonstrated in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref28" id="context_ref_28_3">[28]</a>. In contrast to typical Class-E oscillator circuit applications, the scenario considered in this work calls for low output power operation while maintaining a high efficiency. In <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref29" id="context_ref_29_3">[29]</a>, an active antenna oscillator powered by a solar cell was proposed based on the Class-E oscillator topology of <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref27" id="context_ref_27_3">[27]</a>. The circuit had a 43% efficiency at 905 MHz for an output power of 2 mW. The device was biased at 1.5 V with a collector current of 3.2 mA. Harmonic-balance simulation was used to ensure the oscillating condition and optimize the output power and efficiency of the circuit. This topology was used as a starting point in order to implement a reflection amplifier. It should be noted that based on the results of <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref27" id="context_ref_27_3">[27]</a> and <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref28" id="context_ref_28_3">[28]</a>, higher efficiency values are considered possible, as well as alternative designs based on devices other than the BFT25A silicon npn transistor used in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref29" id="context_ref_29_3">[29]</a>.</p><p>The collector bias of the BFT25A device has been reduced until the oscillation is extinguished while maintaining a negative resistance in order to ensure amplifier gain. The circuit schematic of the amplifier with the component values is shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig6" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 6</a>, while a photograph of the fabricated prototype on a Rogers RO4003C substrate is shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig7" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 7</a>. Since the amplifier is targeting very long-range applications (order of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{\hbox{50}}\sim {\hbox {100}}~{\hbox{m}}</span><script type="math/tex">{\hbox{50}}\sim {\hbox {100}}~{\hbox{m}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, as in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref14" id="context_ref_14_3">[14]</a>), it has been tuned to maximize its gain at very low input power levels, below <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">- {\hbox{30 dBm}}</span><script type="math/tex">- {\hbox{30 dBm}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. The frequency region of operation is the UHF industrial–scientific–medical (ISM) band of 900–930 MHz.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig7"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/6971048/6939737/6939737-fig-7-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/6971048/6939737/6939737-fig-7-small.gif" alt="Figure 7" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 7. </b><fig><p>Fabricated prototypes. (left) phase-shift modulator. (right) reflection amplifier.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/6939737/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<div class="figure figure-full" id="fig8"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/6971048/6939737/6939737-fig-8-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/6971048/6939737/6939737-fig-8-small.gif" alt="Figure 8" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 8. </b><fig><p>Reflection amplifier gain measurements for different input power levels and DC bias voltage values.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/6939737/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<p></p><p>The reflection gain of the amplifier has been measured with a vector network analyzer (VNA) for several input power levels and bias voltages. The maximum bias voltage used is 0.755 V. In <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig8" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 8</a>, the obtained reflection gain values <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">(S_{11})</span><script type="math/tex">(S_{11})</script></tex-math></inline-formula> for different bias points and input power levels are shown. It is important to note that the input power levels shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig8" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 8</a> correspond to the highest input power that can be injected to the amplifier for a given bias voltage without starting any oscillation; i.e., if this input power level is exceeded, an oscillation will occur. A maximum gain of 10.2 dB is achieved for an input power level of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">-{\hbox{50 dBm}}</span><script type="math/tex">-{\hbox{50 dBm}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, when the amplifier is biased at 0.755 V. For lower input power levels, the reflection gain remains on the same level, while the measured current consumption is <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{\hbox{431}}~\mu{\hbox {A}}</span><script type="math/tex">{\hbox{431}}~\mu{\hbox {A}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, resulting in 0.325 mW of dissipated power (<a ref-type="table" anchor="table1" class="fulltext-link">Table I</a>). This low power level could be supplied by low-cost solar cells, even in low-light illumination conditions. For the same bias voltage and a higher input power level of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">-{\hbox{40 dBm}}</span><script type="math/tex">-{\hbox{40 dBm}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, the gain will be slightly reduced by <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\sim {\hbox {1 dB}}</span><script type="math/tex">\sim {\hbox {1 dB}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. During laboratory experiments, it was observed that at a power level of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">-{\hbox{40 dBm}}</span><script type="math/tex">-{\hbox{40 dBm}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> a gain of more than 9 dB can be achieved, but spontaneous interference in the 900–930-MHz band will instantaneously increase the power injected to the amplifier, which starts oscillating. Such interference exists due to nearby RFID readers in the building and other active transmitters in the ISM band. However, in an interference-free environment (e.g., outdoor sensor network deployments, far from domestic infrastructure), a stable operation could be guaranteed. To immune the system from interference and eliminate the probability of starting an oscillation at <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">-{\hbox{40 dBm}}</span><script type="math/tex">-{\hbox{40 dBm}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, the bias voltage has been decreased to 0.745 V.<a ref-type="fn" anchor="fn1" class="footnote-link"><sup>1</sup></a> The maximum gain in this case is <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\sim {\hbox {7.7}}~{\hbox {dB}}</span><script type="math/tex">\sim {\hbox {7.7}}~{\hbox {dB}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and the current consumption is <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{{\hbox{440}}}~\mu{\hbox {A}}</span><script type="math/tex">{{\hbox{440}}}~\mu{\hbox {A}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. For an input power level of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">-{\hbox{30 dBm}}</span><script type="math/tex">-{\hbox{30 dBm}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and an even more reduced bias voltage of 0.732 V, the gain reaches 4.9 dB and the consumption is <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{\hbox{445}}~\mu{\hbox {A}}</span><script type="math/tex">{\hbox{445}}~\mu{\hbox {A}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. The consumption remains on the sub-milliampere region even for higher input power levels (up to 0 dBm); however, since the amplifier is optimized for low-input power operation, the reflection gain for such power levels decreases significantly. Finally, it is noted that the quiescent current when no RF power is applied at the amplifier's input is <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{{\hbox{306}}}~\mu{\hbox {A}}</span><script type="math/tex">{{\hbox{306}}}~\mu{\hbox {A}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. Notice that while the input power increases, the amplifier experiences a gain compression. This effect is anticipated and it has been reported in the literature <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref21" id="context_ref_21_3">[21]</a>.
</p><div class="figure figure-full table" id="table1"><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Table I </b>Reflection amplifier consumption<div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></div><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/6971048/6939737/6939737-table-1-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/6971048/6939737/6939737-table-1-small.gif" alt="Table I" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div></div>
<p></p><p>Some indicative values for frequency, gain values 10–14 dB, bias voltage, and power dissipation are given in <a ref-type="table" anchor="table2" class="fulltext-link">Table II</a> for different reflection amplifiers. The amplifiers used in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref19" id="context_ref_19_3">[19]</a> and <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref20" id="context_ref_20_3">[20]</a> that are used for tagging applications, similar to the tagging/sensing applications this work's system is targeted, require significantly higher bias voltage of over 2 V for comparable gain values and their power dissipation is on the order of milliwatts. The amplifier in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref21" id="context_ref_21_3">[21]</a> is used for analog relaying and is implemented in 130-nm low-power CMOS technology and achieves an ultra-low-power consumption of 0.120 mW. Although this work's amplifier is built using discrete components, its consumption of 0.325 mW is on the same order of magnitude with that of the low-power CMOS circuit.
</p><div class="figure figure-full table" id="table2"><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Table II </b>Reflection amplifier comparison<div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></div><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/6971048/6939737/6939737-table-2-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/6971048/6939737/6939737-table-2-small.gif" alt="Table II" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div></div>
<p></p></div>
<div class="section" id="sec4"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION IV.</div><h2>Phase-Shift Modulator Design</h2></div><p>A phase-shift modulator is designed as a two-port device that selectively delays the signal by 90° between port 1 and port 2, or passes the signal from port 1 to port 2 with no delay. As expressed mathematically in <a ref-type="sec" anchor="sec2" class="fulltext-link">Section II</a>, in round-trip the reflected signal will be shifted by 180° or 0°, achieving a BPSK modulation.</p><p>A delay line of total length <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\lambda/4</span><script type="math/tex">\lambda/4</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is designed in Agilent ADS microwave software and simulated with Agilent Momentum by including the substrate parameters. The line length of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\lambda/4</span><script type="math/tex">\lambda/4</script></tex-math></inline-formula> at the center frequency of 915 MHz corresponds to a delay of 90°, compared to a zero-length line, which corresponds to 0° (practically, on the order of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{\hbox{3}}^{\circ}\sim {\hbox {5}}^{\circ}</span><script type="math/tex">{\hbox{3}}^{\circ}\sim {\hbox {5}}^{\circ}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>). For selecting between the zero-length line and the delay line, two single-pole double-throw (SPDT) switches are utilized (<a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig9" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 9</a>). The SPDT switches will introduce a slight phase change, which does not affect the result since the phase change introduced will be approximately the same for both paths. In that way, the delay-line path will always be 90° shifted compared to the zero-length path.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig9"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/6971048/6939737/6939737-fig-9-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/6971048/6939737/6939737-fig-9-small.gif" alt="Figure 9" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 9. </b><fig><p>Delay-line phase-shift modulator block diagram.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/6939737/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<p></p><p>
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig10"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/6971048/6939737/6939737-fig-10-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/6971048/6939737/6939737-fig-10-small.gif" alt="Figure 10" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 10. </b><fig><p>Transmission coefficient values of the implemented binary phase-shift modulator. state 0: 90° delay. state 1: no delay.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/6939737/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<div class="figure figure-full" id="fig11"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/6971048/6939737/6939737-fig-11-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/6971048/6939737/6939737-fig-11-small.gif" alt="Figure 11" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 11. </b><fig><p>Measurements of phase-shift modulator insertion loss and return loss for state 0 (90° delay) and state 1 (no delay).</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/6939737/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
The phase-shift modulator is implemented on a Rogers RO4003C double copper-clad laminate with dielectric constant <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\epsilon_{r}=3.55</span><script type="math/tex">\epsilon_{r}=3.55</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and loss tangent <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\tan\delta = 0.0021</span><script type="math/tex">\tan\delta = 0.0021</script></tex-math></inline-formula> (<a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig7" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 7</a>). The substrate thickness of 20 mil helps keeping the microstrip lines in reasonable widths on the order of 40 mil for 915 MHz. Two ADG918 SPDT switches are used, which are operated with microampere current consumption at 1.6 V. The switches have been tested for use with voltages on the order of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{\hbox{1}}\sim {\hbox {1.2 V}}</span><script type="math/tex">{\hbox{1}}\sim {\hbox {1.2 V}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> with a penalty of slightly higher insertion loss. The SPDTs are controlled by a single pin with a voltage that can be as low as 0.8 V.<p></p><p>The phase-shifter transmission coefficient <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">S_{21}</span><script type="math/tex">S_{21}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is measured with a VNA to characterize the insertion loss and phase shifting in the two modulator states. In <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig10" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 10</a>, it can be seen that <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{\vert S_{21}\vert \approx 0.79}</span><script type="math/tex">{\vert S_{21}\vert \approx 0.79}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, and the phase difference between the two states is <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\sim {\hbox {87}}^{\circ}</span><script type="math/tex">\sim {\hbox {87}}^{\circ}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. The performance of the phase-shift modulator is acceptable, while the transmission coefficient amplitude could be increased by using lower insertion-loss switches. The return loss and insertion loss for both modulator states can be seen in logarithmic scale in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig11" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 11</a>. The insertion loss for both states is approximately 2 dB (the two states differ by less than 0.1 dB), while the return loss is better than 21 dB for both states, indicating a good matching. Notice that from <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig5" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 5</a>, for the reflection amplifier gain of 10.2 dB, the implemented phase-shifter loss on the order of 2 dB is marginally acceptable. Nevertheless, the modulator is sufficient for the purpose of demonstrating the proof-of-concept of a reflection-amplifier phase-shift modulator, and the insertion loss can be minimized by using lower loss SPDT switches, and utilizing a single-board design to eliminate the SMA connector losses.</p></div>
<div class="section" id="sec5"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION V.</div><h2>System Characterization</h2></div><p>The amplifier phase-shift modulator system is characterized by connecting the two elements (<a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig7" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 7</a>) and measuring the reflection coefficient for the two states. <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig12" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 12</a> shows the two reflection coefficients between 900–930 MHz for the amplifier-equipped tag, at a system input power of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">-{\hbox{40 dBm}}</span><script type="math/tex">-{\hbox{40 dBm}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. At State 0, the reflection coefficient around 915 MHz is approximately <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{\hbox{2}}\angle{\hbox{15}}^{\circ}</span><script type="math/tex">{\hbox{2}}\angle{\hbox{15}}^{\circ}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, while at State 1, the reflection coefficient is approximately <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{\hbox{2.1}}\angle{\hbox{197}}^{\circ}</span><script type="math/tex">{\hbox{2.1}}\angle{\hbox{197}}^{\circ}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. The corresponding phase difference is 182°. The reflection-coefficient amplitude variation across the 900–930-MHz band is on the order of 5%, which is sufficiently low for not affecting the antipodal constellation.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig12"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/6971048/6939737/6939737-fig-12-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/6971048/6939737/6939737-fig-12-small.gif" alt="Figure 12" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 12. </b><fig><p>Measured antipodal reflection-coefficient values <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">(S_{11})</span><script type="math/tex">(S_{11})</script></tex-math></inline-formula> for the two tag modulation states (<inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">-{\hbox{40-dBm}}</span><script type="math/tex">-{\hbox{40-dBm}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> input power).</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/6939737/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<p></p><p>For comparison, a modulator-only tag has been tested, by shorting port 2 of the modulator and measuring the reflection coefficient at the two states. The points can be observed inside the unitary circle, having an amplitude of approximately 0.5 for State 0 and 0.6 for State 1. The phase difference of the two states is approximately 185°, corresponding to a BPSK constellation, with significantly lower SNR than the amplifier-equipped tag constellation.</p><p>The reflection-coefficient difference is plotted across the frequency band in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig13" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 13</a> for the amplifier-equipped tag and the modulator-only tag. It can be seen that the amplifier-equipped tag achieves a <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\vert \Delta \Gamma \vert \approx 4.1</span><script type="math/tex">\vert \Delta \Gamma \vert \approx 4.1</script></tex-math></inline-formula> at 915 MHz, while the modulator-only tag achieves a <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\vert \Delta \Gamma \vert \approx 1.1</span><script type="math/tex">\vert \Delta \Gamma \vert \approx 1.1</script></tex-math></inline-formula>.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig13"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/6971048/6939737/6939737-fig-13-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/6971048/6939737/6939737-fig-13-small.gif" alt="Figure 13" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 13. </b><fig><p>Comparison of reflection-coefficient distance between the two tag states for a conventional modulator-only tag and an enhanced backscatter efficiency amplifier-equipped tag (<inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">-{\hbox{40-dBm}}</span><script type="math/tex">-{\hbox{40-dBm}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> input power).</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/6939737/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<div class="figure figure-full" id="fig14"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/6971048/6939737/6939737-fig-14-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/6971048/6939737/6939737-fig-14-small.gif" alt="Figure 14" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 14. </b><fig><p>Measured backscattered spectrum of a conventional tag without a reflection amplifier and an amplifier-equipped tag. Both tags are excited with a 915-mhz cw and continuously switch between two modulation states with a 250-khz rate, thus backscattering a 250-khz subcarrier.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/6939737/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<p></p><p>Since the tag SNR is proportional to <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\vert \Delta \Gamma \vert^{2}</span><script type="math/tex">\vert \Delta \Gamma \vert^{2}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> (<a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref12" id="context_ref_12_5">[12]</a>, <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref14" id="context_ref_14_5">[14]</a>), this translates to an SNR gain of the proposed tag over the modulator-only tag of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">(4.1/1.1)^{2} = 13.89 \rightarrow 11.43~{\hbox {dB}}</span><script type="math/tex">(4.1/1.1)^{2} = 13.89 \rightarrow 11.43~{\hbox {dB}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>.</p><p>A setup consisting of a signal generator, a spectrum analyzer, and a circulator is used to test the backscattering of the implemented tag. The signal generator outputs a continuous wave (CW) at 915 MHz at <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">-{\hbox{40 dBm}}</span><script type="math/tex">-{\hbox{40 dBm}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. The tag is attached to the circulator so that it is excited by the generator CW, and its backscattering is captured by the spectrum analyzer.</p><p>Notice that measuring the carrier (central frequency) power level with and without an amplifier is not sufficient for characterization due to: 1) leakage of carrier power towards the spectrum analyzer due to low circulator isolation and 2) the tag reflection at the carrier frequency may have such a phase that will create a destructive addition with the carrier signal from the generator. This can lead to a wrong assessment of the system backscattering efficiency. For that reason, a square waveform is applied to the modulator control pin, switching between the 0° and 180° states with a rate of 250 kHz. The tag then backscatters a subcarrier 250 kHz away from the carrier (915.25 MHz), whose power level is measured without and with the amplifier. The power level of the subcarrier is directly related to <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\vert \Delta \Gamma \vert^{2}</span><script type="math/tex">\vert \Delta \Gamma \vert^{2}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, as shown in the backscatter signal model in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref14" id="context_ref_14_5">[14]</a>.</p><p>In <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig14" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 14</a>, the spectra of the two cases can be seen. Notice that the subcarrier power level increases by 8 dB when the amplifier is present compared to the modulator-only case. This gives a proof of the system operation in low input power. The noise floor has been measured to increase by 1 dB on average. This results to a subcarrier SNR increase of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">8-1 = 7~{\hbox {dB}}</span><script type="math/tex">8-1 = 7~{\hbox {dB}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. An SNR increase of 7 dB can have a significant impact in the tag demodulation by the reader. For example, in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref14" id="context_ref_14_5">[14]</a>, it can be seen that for backscatter FSK, an SNR value of 0 dB corresponds to a BER value of 33%. In practice, this tag's information is lost by the reader. However, with a 7-dB increase in SNR, the BER drops down to 6%, which is significantly lower.</p><p>Furthermore, notice the low-frequency nonlinearities around the carrier peak, which could cause interference if the tag signal spectrum is close to the carrier. In practice, the frequency region around the carrier is heavily affected by RF clutter due to low-frequency multipath reflections from the surrounding environments and electronics nonlinearities <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref30" id="context_ref_30_5">[30]</a>. The nonlinearities introduced by the amplifier will further contribute to the RF clutter, which is a frequency region that should be avoided, due to the increased noise floor. Experiments in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref31" id="context_ref_31_5">[31]</a> have shown that the RF clutter might occupy a bandwidth of up to 100 kHz, and low-SNR tag signals in that frequency region may be completely “hidden” from the reader. Properly selecting high subcarrier values can move the tag signal away from the carrier clutter, and guarantee efficient demodulation of low-SNR tag signals.</p><p>The theoretical range increase given an SNR increase of 8 dB can be approximated by using a far-field two-ray path loss model that accounts for a line-of-sight path and a strong reflection from the ground with the formula <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref32" id="context_ref_32_5">[32]</a> <disp-formula id="deqn19" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{\rm PL}(d) = G_{T} + G_{R} + 20\log_{10}\left({{h_{T}~h_{R}}\over{d^{2}}} \right) \eqno{\hbox{(19)}}</span><script type="math/tex; mode=display">{\rm PL}(d) = G_{T} + G_{R} + 20\log_{10}\left({{h_{T}~h_{R}}\over{d^{2}}} \right) \eqno{\hbox{(19)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">{\rm PL}(d) = G_{T} + G_{R} + 20\log_{10}\left({{h_{T}~h_{R}}\over{d^{2}}} \right) \eqno{\hbox{(19)}}</span></span></disp-formula>where <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">G_{T}, G_{R}</span><script type="math/tex">G_{T}, G_{R}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> are the tag antenna and reader antenna gain values, respectively, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">h_{T}, h_{R}</span><script type="math/tex">h_{T}, h_{R}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> are the heights of the tag and reader antenna placement with respect to ground, and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">d</span><script type="math/tex">d</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the distance between the tag and the reader.
